    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }

      AppleID.auth.init({

<span id="freeTextContainer6429122221812398024">The Carls just appeared.<br/><br/>Roaming through New York City at three AM, twenty-three-year-old April May stumbles across a giant sculpture. Delighted by its appearance and craftsmanship—like a ten-foot-tall Transformer wearing a suit of samurai armor—April and her best friend, Andy, make a video with it, which Andy uploads to YouTube. The next day, April wakes up to a viral vide</span>
  <span id="freeText6429122221812398024" style="display:none">The Carls just appeared.<br /><br />Roaming through New York City at three AM, twenty-three-year-old April May stumbles across a giant sculpture. Delighted by its appearance and craftsmanship—like a ten-foot-tall Transformer wearing a suit of samurai armor—April and her best friend, Andy, make a video with it, which Andy uploads to YouTube. The next day, April wakes up to a viral video and a new life. News quickly spreads that there are Carls in dozens of cities around the world—from Beijing to Buenos Aires—and April, as their first documentarian, finds herself at the center of an intense international media spotlight.<br /><br />Seizing the opportunity to make her mark on the world, April now has to deal with the consequences her new particular brand of fame has on her relationships, her safety, and her own identity. And all eyes are on April to figure out not just what the Carls are, but what they want from us.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6429122221812398024" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

      $('span_class_gr-hyperlink_sort_order_span_tip_631').observe('prototip:hidden', function () {
        $$('span.elementTwo').each(function (e) {
          if (e.getStyle('display') !== 'none') {
            var lessLink = e.next();
            swapContent(lessLink);
          }
        });
      });

//]]>
</script>    </div>

<div id="review_2551358279" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2551358279" />
    <a title="chai ♡" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/42130592-chai"><img alt="chai ♡" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1603167303p2/42130592.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2551358279?book_show_action=true">Oct 04, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="chai ♡" class="user" itemprop="url" name="chai ♡" href="/user/show/42130592-chai">chai ♡</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/42130592-chai?shelf=lgbtq-rep">lgbtq-rep</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/42130592-chai?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/42130592-chai?shelf=lgbtq-ff">lgbtq-ff</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/42130592-chai?shelf=poc-rep">poc-rep</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/42130592-chai?shelf=adult-contemporary">adult-contemporary</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/42130592-chai?shelf=adult">adult</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/42130592-chai?shelf=read-in-2018">read-in-2018</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/42130592-chai?shelf=adult-sff">adult-sff</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/42130592-chai?shelf=lgbtq-bi">lgbtq-bi</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2551358279" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1472786480432309034">April May, a twenty-three-year-old bisexual art-school grad languishing in a Manhattan startup, inadvertently makes first contact with an alien when she happens upon a ten-feet tall Transformers-like sculpture. She calls her YouTuber bestfriend Andy and together they upload a faux-serious interview with the statue which they dub Carl, with no way of knowing that the curtain is about to rise on a drama of their own invention when the video goes exponentially viral and many Carls materialize in ci</span>
  <span id="freeText1472786480432309034" style="display:none">April May, a twenty-three-year-old bisexual art-school grad languishing in a Manhattan startup, inadvertently makes first contact with an alien when she happens upon a ten-feet tall Transformers-like sculpture. She calls her YouTuber bestfriend Andy and together they upload a faux-serious interview with the statue which they dub Carl, with no way of knowing that the curtain is about to rise on a drama of their own invention when the video goes exponentially viral and many Carls materialize in cities around the world.<br /><br />The unascertained intention of the Carls, enormous as it loomed, was not the only thing weighing on April’s mind. She is now a celebrity, hated and loved with equal ferocity, and when people start getting besieged by perplexing dreams of the Carls making, April takes upon herself the inconvenience of persuading everyone that the Carls are a peaceful entity and not whatever maleficent meaning many want to suit to their existence.<br /><br />April soon finds out that the fame that tied her to the Carls made the world—and maybe even her closest friends—love her a little less. Closeness to one, it seems, means distance from the other. And celebrity comes with a price.<br /><br /><blockquote>
  <em>A year ago, I watched the world fall in love with my best friend. We thought it would be fun, we thought it would be silly, but then that love tore her apart and put her back together different. April and I, alone in a hotel room, plotted to change her from a person into a story. It worked. It worked because it was a great story, and one that fit her. We did not know that she would actually become it. The most insidious part of fame for April wasn’t that other people dehumanized her; it was that she dehumanized herself.</em>
</blockquote><br /><br /><strong>I couldn’t put this book down.</strong> I pursued the plot blindly, like in a dream compelled by some great mysterious force to move forward. This is not a thriller by any means, and not what I'd call a page-turner. There was no urgency in my reading, no overwhelming desire to see what happens next—yet I found myself deeply engrossed and utterly content to spend more time with a story where the supernatural felt genuinely weird, a little off-putting, and entirely seductive. Hank’s simplistic and often overly conversational writing style is not for everyone, but it worked for me. It’s also full of lengthy passages of technical exposition about everything from physics to neurology, which can get too leaden at times, but I think Hank’s sheer joy in imparting these ideas beams through like a laser more often than not.<br /><br />Our protagonist, April May, is the narrator, and her first-person account is…full of personality. She’s what you would call "very unlikeable", but in the way an unlikeable character who doesn’t soften up or sugarcoats the less than pleasant aspects of their personalities and with whom you can—to your great horror—assimilate would be. Her character is lackadaisically wry, flawed, potentially unreliable, but at the core benevolent and well-intentioned and always immensely engaging.<br /><br />Sure, I grappled with the impulse to yell at her countless times when her selfish confidence pushes past refreshing and well into repellent and puts everyone around her in danger. But I understood the extraordinary dread of desperately wanting to be a part of something extraordinary and watching the opportunity slip through your fingers. I also felt for her when everyone begun to strip away everything she was until it was small enough to fit into the story they’ve made up about her. More than anything—and I hate to admit this—I’ve come to realize that the things I disliked about her were things I disliked about myself: the unnameable need to be <em>liked</em>, the kind of selfishness that is putting up so many walls around yourself that you can’t see anyone or anything beyond your own problems, and sometimes being—for lack of an apter term—a spectacularily <em>shitty</em> friend.<br /><br />But Hank not only weaves together a suspenseful tale of April's involvement with an alien sculpture and her quest to figure out its origin and intent, he also does so with sly social commentary, and, for me, that was the best part.<br /><br /><strong>Hank Green created a story with great deep undercurrents.</strong> Under the surface is a very forward and honest discussion about social media and the uncomfortable commodification of the self it perpetuates, and a reminder that a person’s online presence is only a fraction of their personhood, and how we all—knowingly or unknowingly—peddle out every profitable aspects of our personalities on the internet for the very attention most of us would hate to receive in real life. The insights Green drops in through his characters about the process of suddenly finding oneself to be internet famous are also sharp and perfect. Especially how trying to find yourself through your feed and measuring your self-esteem on likes and comments eventually creates an alternate version of yourself that you can only attain at the price of laborious efforts, and that ultimately obfuscates your very sense of self and puts you between a fantasized—but not less real—you behind a screen, and the real you, who becomes more and more fictional.<br /><br />This book also hammers at social-media for glamourizing and rewarding the worst of human attributes (vanity, exaggerated self-importance, materialism, deception, envy, ostentation, narcissism, superiority, etc...) and conditioning people into believing that any of these traits are positive or favorable. Hank is serving some seriously scorching tea about social media, let me tell you.<br /><br />Which is why it’s difficult not to be disappointed by the book's second-half shift away from real relationships with clear and present stakes in favor of pursuing a meandering plot that builds up to what I think was a trite ending, but it's a disappointment experienced mostly in retrospect because, as it turns out, this is not a standalone. And I'm really intrigued to see where and how this story unfolds.<br /><br />Moreover, This book was quite frankly more diverse than any of John Green's books [insert the I SAID WHAT I SAID gif meme]: April is bisexual and her girlfriend Maya is a sapphic black woman. Hank also calls out white privilege and bi-erasure.<br /><br />Overall, this was quite a riveting, witty book, and I was thoroughly absorbed!<br /><br /><a href="https://runawaywithdreamthieves.wordpress.com" rel="nofollow noopener">BLOG</a> | <a href="https://twitter.com/kzabrekker" rel="nofollow noopener">TWITTER</a> | <a href="https://instagram.com/runawaywithdreamthieves" rel="nofollow noopener">INSTAGRAM</a> | <a href="https://azqaban.tumblr.com" rel="nofollow noopener">TUMBLR</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="1472786480432309034" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

  <span id="freeText3370219855142746227" style="display:none">Publisher: So Hank you want to write a book?<br />Hank: Yes, my brother wrote a bunch which means that I can too!<br />Publisher: What do you want it to be about.<br />Hank: I want to write a book about aliens.<br />Publisher: So like the 5th wave.<br />Hank: Kind of but let's remove all the violent parts.<br />Publisher: So like E.T.?<br />Hank: The alien won't actually talk or interact with the humans. <div id="review_2583641223" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2583641223" />
    <a title="Zoë" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/9303625-zo"><img alt="Zoë" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1580008879p2/9303625.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2583641223?book_show_action=true">Nov 10, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Zoë" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Zoë" href="/user/show/9303625-zo">Zoë</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9781524743444" href="/book/show/38186611-an-absolutely-remarkable-thing">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2583641223" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5746579564449125478"><u>Original 2018 Review</u><br/><b>4.5/5</b><br/>I'm honestly still in shock. I went into this book quite skeptically as this is Hank Green's debut coupled with the fact that I don't tend to gravitate towards sci-fi. <br/>HOWEVER.<br/>This was such a whirlwind of a book! I didn't research this book before starting (which I'd recommend - just read it), so it took a little bit to get my footing in the story. After that, though WOW. Constant twists, turns, and a mystery that keeps unfolding. I simply <i>had</i> to keep reading to figure o</span>
  <span id="freeText5746579564449125478" style="display:none"><u>Original 2018 Review</u><br /><b>4.5/5</b><br />I'm honestly still in shock. I went into this book quite skeptically as this is Hank Green's debut coupled with the fact that I don't tend to gravitate towards sci-fi. <br />HOWEVER.<br />This was such a whirlwind of a book! I didn't research this book before starting (which I'd recommend - just read it), so it took a little bit to get my footing in the story. After that, though WOW. Constant twists, turns, and a mystery that keeps unfolding. I simply <i>had</i> to keep reading to figure out what was up with Carl.<br />But I think my favorite aspect were the two discussions about fame as well as mistrust of change/"outsiders." The first topic was especially interesting to me as it was written by an online creator and, being one myself (though a much smaller one), I could semi-relate to it.<br />Despite my excitement for this story, I had to dock off a little because I felt like Hank Green's writing from a woman's perspective felt a <i>little</i> off. I also felt like the supporting characters could have been fleshed out more, but maybe that was purposeful as April is definitely self-centered.<br />Anyway, catch me first in line for the sequel!</span>
  <a data-text-id="5746579564449125478" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781524744137" href="/book/show/41973294-an-absolutely-remarkable-thing">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/75831724-mary-s-r?shelf=adult">adult</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/75831724-mary-s-r?shelf=lgbt">lgbt</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/75831724-mary-s-r?shelf=literary-fiction">literary-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/75831724-mary-s-r?shelf=contemporary">contemporary</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/75831724-mary-s-r?shelf=all-time-faves">all-time-faves</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/75831724-mary-s-r?shelf=new-adult">new-adult</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/75831724-mary-s-r?shelf=faves">faves</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/75831724-mary-s-r?shelf=sociology">sociology</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/75831724-mary-s-r?shelf=science-fiction">science-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/75831724-mary-s-r?shelf=mystery">mystery</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2592813002" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer70418299151519802">When we hear <b>robots</b> or <b>aliens</b>, we first think of war and mass destruction. That right there is, to me, <i>science fiction</i>’s gravest, most unforgivable crime against understanding and progress—and <i>Hank Green</i> agrees.<br/><br/><blockquote>
  <i>“Even on this most terrible of days, even when the worst of us are all we can think of, I am proud to be a human.”</i>
</blockquote><br/><img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1560187480i/27647421._SX540_.jpg' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/><br/>If I want to talk about about why <b><i>An Absolutely Remarkable Thing</i> is an absolutely remarkable work of fiction, blending First Encounter, thriller, mystery, and humour with </b></span>
  <span id="freeText70418299151519802" style="display:none">When we hear <b>robots</b> or <b>aliens</b>, we first think of war and mass destruction. That right there is, to me, <i>science fiction</i>’s gravest, most unforgivable crime against understanding and progress—and <i>Hank Green</i> agrees.<br><br><blockquote>
  <i>“Even on this most terrible of days, even when the worst of us are all we can think of, I am proud to be a human.”</i>
</blockquote><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1560187480i/27647421._SX540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>If I want to talk about about why <b><i>An Absolutely Remarkable Thing</i> is an absolutely remarkable work of fiction, blending First Encounter, thriller, mystery, and humour with sociology and deep insights on fame and social media</b>, I will first need to test your (<i>I sincerely hope not limited</i>) patience and tell you about <i>speculative fiction</i>.<br><br>This is based on my own limited knowledge and could contain errors: <i>speculative fiction</i> was originally penned to include a general category of genres such as <i>fantasy</i> and <i>science fiction</i>—you know, anything that crosses a line in the fiction area and heads towards bending reality. That is, until <i>Robert Heinlein</i>, in an attempt to put an end to the arguments on the actual genre of his books, declared them as <i>speculative fiction</i>. Consequently, now the term describes works that ask a <i>what if</i> question followed by an unlikely, abstract situation, and answer it with a <i>people would respond in this way</i> speculation.<br><br>That is exactly what <i>Hank Green</i> does.<br><br><blockquote>
  <i>“When you’re faced with something you don’t understand, I think the most natural thing but also the least interesting thing you can be is afraid.”</i>
</blockquote><br>AART is an extremely weird (<i>in the best way</i>) work of speculative fiction because <b>it takes the traditional alien + artificial intelligence Sci-Fi scenario and, even while conserving its epic and world-scale quality, breaks it down to a more realistic script through inserting it into an advanced contemporary society</b> and asking <i>how would a modern Earth react to stumbling across an otherworldly, static robot popping up on the street corners all over the world?</i><br><br><blockquote>
  <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1597502914i/29972268._SX540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
</blockquote><br>What role would celebrities, media influencers, and political pundits play in guiding the planet to choose a course of action regarding this world-changing mystery? And which path would they choose: acceptance or fear, solidarity or prejudice, peace or violence, order or chaos?<br><br><blockquote>
  <i>“I’m not afraid of them, I’m afraid of their fear.”</i>
</blockquote><br>Fear, prejudice, violence, chaos...they’re all much easier than their opposites. And, I admit, the aforementioned scenario <i>is</i> rather scary; indeed, pee-your-pants, stay-up-all-night, everything-is-a-lie, what-has-my-life-even-been scary. So the fight for unity and understanding of the existence and role of fear in the face of uncertainty among us humans as a whole species as well as unique, seperate individuals is unequivocally worthy of discussion.<br><br>Fortunately for any fellow questioning soul, Hank, too, is interested. And his AART is <b>a perceptive tale of facing and embracing the unknown; of accepting the people hailing from far and wide as fellow humans seperate from solitary brands and ideas. What it is, is a profoundly human tale of reality despite its (<i>presently</i>) unreal concept that truly does a number on your heart.</b><br><br><blockquote>
  <i>What is reality except for the things that people universally experience the same way?</i>
</blockquote><br><blockquote>
  <blockquote>
    <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1597503249i/29972289._SY540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
  </blockquote>
</blockquote><br><br><b>
  <u>Humanity, the <i>Absolutely Remarkable Thing</i> (ART)</u>
</b><br><br><blockquote>
  <i>“This is what humanity is, solidarity in the face of fear. Hope in the face of destruction.”</i>
</blockquote><br>As you stroll through the streets of your life, you’d most probably glimpse pieces of art strown about you—a painting on the wall, a dress behind the glass, a statue in the corner. We all see them; a few of us barely give any a second glance; most of us pause and praise and then move ahead, forgetting in a blink; but a very rare portion stay to drink in its beauty and, no matter how far they go, they never truly leave it behind.<br><br>In the eyes of <i>Hank Green</i>, <b>humanity is yet another addition to the unappreciated gems of the world, perhaps even the most important of them all; humanity is an <i>ART</i>, an <i>Absolutely Remarkable Thing</i>.</b><br><br><blockquote>
  <i>“We are each individuals, but the far greater thing is what we are together, and if that isn’t protected and cherished, we are headed to a bad place.”</i>
</blockquote><br>Humans might be too often selfish and limited to their own tiny windows and perspectives, but that takes nothing away from the sheer neglect of humanity in itself. We might be holier-than-thou individuals that mostly consider themselves higher than animals and all other beings, but <b>our love is centred on individuals—ourselves, a friend, a family; it’s so very rare to see love of humans as one people of Earth, to see love for a person not just because of who they are but because they are simply and beautifully human.</b><br><br>Human, because we feel and we fight and, however foolish, however doomed, we do not give up. Annnnd that’s enough sentimentality for one day, moving on—this book does weird things to me.<br><br><br><blockquote>
  <blockquote>
    <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1597503055i/29972272._SY540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
  </blockquote>
</blockquote><br><br><b>
  <u>Power &amp; Media, the <i>Alluringly Sanctimonious Swamp</i> (ASS)</u>
</b><br><br><blockquote>
  <i>Just because someone has power over you doesn’t mean they’re going to use it to hurt you. People who believe that tend to either be:<br><b>1.</b> People who have been victims of that sort of behavior, or...<br><b>2.</b> People who, if given power, will use it to hurt you.</i>
</blockquote><br>Despite Hank’s beautiful vision, to me (<i>as a quite cynical soul with a high endurance for sentimentality</i>) the most absolutely remarkable thing about AART is the concepts and themes: <b>threading the muddy waters of life and fame; the world’s tendency to be distrustful, suspicious, and hateful; the affects of social media on people and people on social media; sexuality and the struggles of being bisexual; racism and, of course, sexism.</b><br><br>Humans are, in short, a mess, viewing the world through one small crack, from one single angle, and judging the whole shape based on that tiny truth even as they don’t see the whole picture—or, really, the different opposing truths. So what happens if these individuals are given power?<br><br><blockquote>
  <i>Human beings are terrible at accepting uncertainty, so when we’re ignorant, we make assumptions based on how we imagine the world. And our guess is so obviously correct that other guesses seem, at best, willful ignorance—at worst, an attack.</i>
</blockquote><br>Social media platforms are a place for this messed up species to interact, communicate, and make a difference. We get to be heard just as we hear. And that gives us power. <b>Power, however, is a trickster that draws you in like a mirage, an overwhelming force that swallows you up like a swamp.</b><br><br>But how does it happen? Why would one succumb to its caress? And, again, what happens when an individual gains such power—a human who, like all humans, enjoys destruction more than creation because, let’s be honest, it’s both easier and more fun to watch? <b>Where would that leave us when reasonable, considerate words are boring in their complexity and angry, offensive rants are attractive in their simplicity?</b><br><br>Answering these questions <i>does</i> make this book less a thriller and more a sociological commentary at times, but it’s wholly worth the time and effort it takes. The humour is certainly a plus. Humour is always a plus, really. The salt to meal, adding taste.<br><br><br><blockquote>
  <blockquote>
    <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1597503550i/29972303._SY540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
  </blockquote>
</blockquote><br><br><b>
  <u>April May, the <i>Acrimoniously Radical Brand</i> (ARB)</u>
</b><br><br><blockquote>
  <i>“You’re a digital girl, April, in a digital world. We all know how to perform.”</i>
</blockquote><br>April May (<i>prepare for the crappiest joke on the face of planet earth</i>) June July August September October November December January February March, yes, April May (<i>okay no that’s actually the crappiest joke anywhere in the multiverse still #sorrynotsorry</i>) is one of those <b>initially frustrating MCs that take a long and awe-inspiring journey of development throughout the novel, swaggering from I-wanna-punch-you right up to oh-my-god-I-adore-you-you-awesome-idiot.</b><br><br>And a core ingredient in her character development is her attempts to build an appealing image of herself for the cameras that, unknowingly, result in her ending up building <i>herself</i> instead of a brand.<br><br><blockquote>
  <i>You can only do so much pretending before you become the thing you’re pretending to be.</i>
</blockquote><br>Okay, let’s back it up a bit. <b>When you’re as attention-seeking, power-hungry, and control-addicted as April and happen care so much about what people think of you, fame and the media and drag you beneath the surface of the lake faster than a freshwater crocodile.</b><br><br>And her sudden celebrity status drowns her so deeply that her worst traits take over the wheel and drive her right towards the cliff. Her charm and charisma allow for her ambition to bear fruit—fruits none other than the growth of her fleeting, inconsiderate, and reckless characteristics and the blooming of that touch of lacking empathy. April is extremely smart, but also extremely stupid; one leads her to the heights of fame, one to the lowest of behaviours towards her loved ones in her fraying relationships. Or lack thereof. Seeing her dynamic with her friends, family, and fans is <b>both distressing and informing when it comes to what power and attention can do to you.</b> But “when you get stuck fighting small battles, it makes you small.”<br><br><blockquote>
  <i>I looked cocky, but people either love that or they love to hate it, and in the attention game (which I was playing even if I didn’t know I was), those things are equally good.</i>
</blockquote><br>So yes, our unlikely hero screws up. But she still plays the games of power well and, in the end, she grows into a moving hurricane of a young woman. She might be voicey, but she’s hilarious. Wild as a cat and strong as a lion, she stole my cold heart. April has her own demons to fight, with as many mistakes as wise choices on her path, and that is what declares her as human.<br><br><blockquote>
  <i>“Behold the field in which I grow my fucks. Lay thine eyes upon it and see that it is barren.”</i>
</blockquote><br><b>What makes her story most interesting though (<i>after the building yourself part, obviously, because I’m me and I do psychology duh</i>) is that it’s written in retrospect, with numerous honest and earnest reflections on and criticisms of her idiocies right next to victories. I won’t deny I’m a sucker for cryptic comments that make you boil with a sense of <i>holy shit holy shit what’s gonna happen #panicking</i>.</b> Well, that’s my crazyawesomeself for you.<br><br>In the end, <i>An Absolutely Remarkable Thing</i> is more the story of humanity and April May rather than humanity and worldwide change because, you know, deliciously self-absorbed main character and all.<br><br><br><blockquote>
  <img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1597503349ra/29972296.gif" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
</blockquote><br><br><i>I am aware that you’re here for an epic tale of intrigue and mystery and adventure and near death and actual death, but in order to get to that (unless you want to skip to chapter 13—I’m not your boss), you’re going to have to deal with the fact that I, April May, in addition to being one of the most important things that has ever happened to the human race, am also a woman in her twenties who has made some mistakes.<br><br>I have the story, and so I get to tell it to you the way I want. That means you get to understand me, not just my story.</i><br><br><br><b>
  <u>Companions</u>
</b><br><br><b>
  <i>Book series playlist:</i>
</b> <a href="https://open.spotify.com/playlist/4B8jAPI9NvZLHyrE7OGSdn?si=qr-b7vH8Qe2WHhxffCa3Yg" rel="nofollow noopener">Spotify URL<br><br><blockquote><blockquote><blockquote><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1597392483i/29964353._SY540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"></blockquote></blockquote></blockquote></a><br><i>
  <b>Books in series:</b>
</i><br>➛ An Absolutely Remarkable Thing (The Carls, #1) ★★★★★<br>➛ <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2694490518" rel="nofollow noopener">A Beautifully Foolish Endeavor</a> (The Carls, #2) ★★★★★</span>
  <a data-text-id="70418299151519802" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

I did not realize this would be part of a series when I picked it up so I was expecting a satisfying ending, and it did not deliver for me. Bummer.</span>

<div id="review_2428946292" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2428946292" />
    <a title="Tammy" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/50603889-tammy"><img alt="Tammy" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1566220700p2/50603889.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2428946292?book_show_action=true">Jul 06, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Tammy" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Tammy" href="/user/show/50603889-tammy">Tammy</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9781524743444" href="/book/show/38186611-an-absolutely-remarkable-thing">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2428946292" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15401447691601287561">April May is the snarky, relationship wrecking narrator who is unwittingly catapulted into the dizzying heights of international fame upon being the first to discover a randomly named “Carl”. Initially thought to be an art installation, the Carls prove to be considerably more than visually striking. April becomes addicted to being first and staying first within the media both social and otherwise. At first glance this seems like a Young Adult novel and it will excite this audience but there is a</span>
  <span id="freeText15401447691601287561" style="display:none">April May is the snarky, relationship wrecking narrator who is unwittingly catapulted into the dizzying heights of international fame upon being the first to discover a randomly named “Carl”. Initially thought to be an art installation, the Carls prove to be considerably more than visually striking. April becomes addicted to being first and staying first within the media both social and otherwise. At first glance this seems like a Young Adult novel and it will excite this audience but there is a lot more going on than the plot might lead you to believe which makes it appealing to more mature readers. In addition to fame (its effects and aftermath), we take a look at gender (identification and fluidity), crowd behavior (physical as well as cyber), and the unification of humanity in order to solve a puzzle. This is a fantastical journey that leads one to an unexpected destination.</span>
  <a data-text-id="15401447691601287561" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2129697977" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2129697977" />
    <a title="Haley" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7196715-haley"><img alt="Haley" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1592591364p2/7196715.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2129697977?book_show_action=true">Sep 20, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Haley" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Haley" href="/user/show/7196715-haley">Haley</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9781524743444" href="/book/show/38186611-an-absolutely-remarkable-thing">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2129697977" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2839397738290683997"><b>Upon Announcement:<br/><br/>I CAN'T WAAAAIT!</b><br/><br/><img src='https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1506088978ra/23987970.gif' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/><br/><b>After Reading:</b><br/><br/>So I am giving this four stars for now, even though it is possible it is a 3/3.5-star book. I really loved everything it had to say and the way in which it said it, and I thought it was a highly original and well-written story, but I was confused and unimpressed by the ending. (That being said, given the premise of the book, I would like to point out the irony of this being easily my most-liked review ever.)<br/><br/>What I did love about this story was its </span>
  <span id="freeText2839397738290683997" style="display:none"><b>Upon Announcement:<br><br>I CAN'T WAAAAIT!</b><br><br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1506088978ra/23987970.gif" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br><b>After Reading:</b><br><br>So I am giving this four stars for now, even though it is possible it is a 3/3.5-star book. I really loved everything it had to say and the way in which it said it, and I thought it was a highly original and well-written story, but I was confused and unimpressed by the ending. (That being said, given the premise of the book, I would like to point out the irony of this being easily my most-liked review ever.)<br><br>What I did love about this story was its focus on fame and the way sudden celebrity—especially internet celebrity—affects a person. April May becomes more brand and spokeswoman than human for much of this book, and because she's the narrator, looking back on past events with new clarity and self-realization, her downward spiral is very clear and honestly understandable. I could totally understand why she made the decisions she did, why so many people hanging onto her every word would be heady, how she couldn't find it in her to stop. And I liked that, looking back, she knew that it should have been obvious she was being destructive and foolish, but how, in the moment, her choices fit her state of mind.<br><br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1538280538ra/26374701.gif" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>I wish we had seen more of Maya, and more of Maya, Andy, and April all together as friends, but I liked the clearly defined characteristics of each person in the story. I thought these were strong characters, with original views, personalities, and voices, and I liked the way they worked together through the problems of the Carls.<br><br>And while the message of the book was a blatant one, at times, it's perfect for the time. Not only that fame changes you, not necessarily for the better; not only how even good people can mistake fame as an opportunity to speak for your entire system of beliefs, and, along the way, lose your personhood; but how we're stronger together than apart. This book was very focused on bringing people together across the globe; April realizes time and time again that working through a problem alone never solves it. It's through endless, global collaboration that we make strides—or even a small collaboration between friends who have different ways of thinking.<br><br>But the <i>ending.</i> WHAT.<br><br><a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[There's no conclusion to this book. After endless Carl puzzles and the world coming together and first contact with <i>aliens,</i> April dies, speaks to Carl for a couple of pages (in which he says she's special without ever saying <i>why</i>), and then the Carls leave and eventually April comes back to life?? Where did she go? Where did the Carls go? Is Hank saying these aliens have the power to <i>resurrect dead people?</i> And HOW, because humans have an entirely different makeup than Carls. Why did it take so long? Why is she back now, and where is the world going from here?<br><br>It felt like I was waiting for a full finish to this story and what it all means and how the world will cope with the disappearance of the Carls and the re-arrival of living, breathing (?) Carl. And I understand that might be too much for one book. But I at least wanted to know <i>why</i> April was alive, <i>why</i> she was chosen, and where she had been all this time.<br><br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1538280538ra/26374703.gif" class="gr-hostedUserImg"> <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> <br><br>I could have lived without a full conclusion for the Carls. I needed a conclusion for April.<br><br>Overall, I love the way this book is laid out. I love how modern it is, and how it's more focused on the big picture and hindsight than characters (even though I am traditionally a huge fan of character-driven stories). I love the originality. I couldn't put it down. I just wanted a few more chapters.<br><br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1538280538ra/26374702.gif" class="gr-hostedUserImg"></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></span>
  <a data-text-id="2839397738290683997" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_2848806634" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2848806634" />
    <a title="Cristina Monica" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/14596170-cristina-monica"><img alt="Cristina Monica" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1589617514p2/14596170.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2848806634?book_show_action=true">Jun 06, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Cristina Monica" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Cristina Monica" href="/user/show/14596170-cristina-monica">Cristina Monica</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/14596170-cristina-monica?shelf=celebrity">celebrity</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/14596170-cristina-monica?shelf=science-fiction">science-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/14596170-cristina-monica?shelf=social-media">social-media</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/14596170-cristina-monica?shelf=mystery">mystery</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/14596170-cristina-monica?shelf=lgbt">lgbt</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2848806634" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9012830843147941051">This book has rekindled my interest in the Science Fiction genre which, after so many past disappointments, was not an easy thing to do. But feeling indifferent about this story was close to impossible, especially with a narrator like April May who enjoys speaking directly to the reader, thus encouraging us to feel involved in whatever happens. I was also rather curious about the ‘‘Carls,’’ these impressive and mysterious sculptures found everywhere around the world that no one can seem to be ab</span>
  <span id="freeText9012830843147941051" style="display:none">This book has rekindled my interest in the Science Fiction genre which, after so many past disappointments, was not an easy thing to do. But feeling indifferent about this story was close to impossible, especially with a narrator like April May who enjoys speaking directly to the reader, thus encouraging us to feel involved in whatever happens. I was also rather curious about the ‘‘Carls,’’ these impressive and mysterious sculptures found everywhere around the world that no one can seem to be able to make heads or tails of. <br /><br />Still, some people try. April May is one of those people and her interest in the Carls is changing her entire life. She goes from anonymous to notorious. She has made it her mission to figure out these Carls—solve the puzzle! I had fun following her adventures and seeing her popularity increase. Fame gets to her head but she’s right… Popularity is addictive. Whether you seek it or not, it will impact you and change the way you behave in the world most likely. April is aware of the effect it has on her and the Carls’ hold on her but she can’t seem to stop. She’s in it too deep now.<br /><br />Forgive me for saying this so often lately but I’ve never read anything like this before. Such a cliché, I know, but I’m so used to being let down by sci-fi that I am over the moon right now because this story made me realize that, yes, sci-fi can be super weird but that if I keep giving it chances and never give up on it, it will surprise me when I least expect it. And that feels so good I almost want to forget all those times I couldn’t finish a story involving advanced technology and the presence of unidentified beings. So please don’t hesitate to pick this up, whether you love sci-fi or not. <br /><br /><a href="https://hitormissbooks.wordpress.com/" rel="nofollow noopener">Blog</a> | <a href="https://www.youtube.com/c/LolaReviewer" rel="nofollow noopener">Youtube</a> | <a>Twitter</a> | <a>Instagram</a> | <a href="https://plus.google.com/u/0/105021852309973899120/posts" rel="nofollow noopener">Google+</a> | <a href="https://www.bloglovin.com/blogs/hit-miss-books-12564715" rel="nofollow noopener">Bloglovin’</a><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="9012830843147941051" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_2645185029" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2645185029" />
    <a title="Larry H" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5872506-larry-h"><img alt="Larry H" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1577826084p2/5872506.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2645185029?book_show_action=true">Dec 30, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Larry H" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Larry H" href="/user/show/5872506-larry-h">Larry H</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2645185029" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13909286274598794669">It was 2:45 in the morning, and April May was walking home in New York City after a long night of working. Out of the corner of her eye, she saw it:<br/><br/>"A ten-foot-tall Transformer wearing a suit of samurai armor, its huge barrel chest lifted up to the sky a good four or five feet above my head. It just stood there in the middle of the sidewalk, full of energy and power. It looked like it might, at any moment, turn and fix that empty, regal stare on <i>me</i>. But instead it just stood there, silent and al</span>
  <span id="freeText13909286274598794669" style="display:none">It was 2:45 in the morning, and April May was walking home in New York City after a long night of working. Out of the corner of her eye, she saw it:<br /><br />"A ten-foot-tall Transformer wearing a suit of samurai armor, its huge barrel chest lifted up to the sky a good four or five feet above my head. It just stood there in the middle of the sidewalk, full of energy and power. It looked like it might, at any moment, turn and fix that empty, regal stare on <i>me</i>. But instead it just stood there, silent and almost scornful, like the world didn't deserve its attention."<br /><br />April decides to call her friend Andy to see if he could take video of this statue, whom she has called Carl. The two record some video footage just to be sure there was some record it existed, and that it wasn't just some prank. They goof around a bit with the statue, post some footage to social media, and crash.<br /><br />What awaits April and Andy the next morning is extraordinary. Not only has their video footage gone viral, but apparently, Carls have shown up in cities all across the world. No one understands what they want or where they came from, but one thing is clear: April and Andy have found themselves at the center of a media frenzy, and April is determined to get out in front of the story, even if it means becoming a more public person than she has ever wanted.<br /><br />"Even Before Carl, I spent time thinking about what I'd say if I ever had a platform to say it. That's what art is about, right? I mean, not app interfaces, but art. Much of the best art is about balancing between reflecting culture while simultaneously being removed from it and commenting on it. In the best case, maybe an artist gets to say something about culture that hasn't been said and needs to be said."<br /><br />The pair connects with Miranda, a scientist, who believes that the Carls are asking for materials—iodine, americium, and uranium. When a small experiment with Carl leads to chaos across the world, people, including the U.S. government, start to worry if the Carls' intentions are positive and/or peaceful, which forces April to realize that there are individuals out there who want to advance their own causes, and will use Carl—and her—as pawns.<br /><br /><b>
  <i>An Absolutely Remarkable Thing</i>
</b> is as much a story about the origins and intentions of the Carls as it is a commentary on our fame-obsessed culture. April discovers, slower than she might have hoped, that while it may be exciting to get everything you've always wanted, to appear on every conceivable television program and talk show, and even have the president's private phone number, there are consequences, which can put your own safety at risk, as well as your relationships, your health, even your future.<br /><br />This book was a little zany for my tastes. I felt like it didn't really know whether it wanted to be more of a sci-fi mystery about the Carls or more of a lampoon of the culture of celebrity, and meshing the two didn't quite work. While there are parts of this book which feel very current, after a while I thought things were getting repetitive and a little bit overly complicated.<br /><br />I'm a big fan of John Green, and his brother Hank definitely shares some storytelling characteristics, as well as a penchant for characters whose primary language is sarcasm. I thought Hank Green had a really interesting idea, but he didn't quite execute it as well as he could have. There are definitely interesting, humorous, and insightful moments here, but overall, the story seemed a little too wacky. <br /><br />See all of my reviews at <a href="http://itseithersadnessoreuphoria.blogspot.com" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener">itseithersadnessoreuphoria.blogspot.com</a>.</span>
  <a data-text-id="13909286274598794669" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2281516598" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2281516598" />
    <a title="C.G. Drews" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/11375137-c-g-drews"><img alt="C.G. Drews" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1521552918p2/11375137.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2281516598?book_show_action=true">Nov 05, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="C.G. Drews" class="user" itemprop="url" name="C.G. Drews" href="/user/show/11375137-c-g-drews">C.G. Drews</a>

          added it

<span id="freeTextContainer6163296842002889483"><b>I obviously wanted to read this because...it's <i>Hank Green.</i>.</b> But I think I will just stick to the other brother of John for now oops. This style of story isn't really in my realm of interests, I confess. Adults/scifi? Hm. So possibly the book is just *not for me* but I was so confused and detached. <br/><br/>Like April is an actual horrible person so why would I care about her? And she gets famous instantly for seeing a robot on a street and making a movie of it. This is BEFORE they think it's an alien. I </span>
  <span id="freeText6163296842002889483" style="display:none"><b>I obviously wanted to read this because...it's <i>Hank Green.</i>.</b> But I think I will just stick to the other brother of John for now oops. This style of story isn't really in my realm of interests, I confess. Adults/scifi? Hm. So possibly the book is just *not for me* but I was so confused and detached. <br /><br />Like April is an actual horrible person so why would I care about her? And she gets famous instantly for seeing a robot on a street and making a movie of it. This is BEFORE they think it's an alien. I don't...get it? Why would people even care (this is prior to the alien part, just keep that in mind). I didn't care. ¯\_(ツ)_/¯ But I think what made me most disenchanted was April just being a total jerk at every opportunity. (Especially to her girlfriend?! Who she continues to use for solving alien puzzles but treated her like utter trash.) And I found the writing really dry tbh.<br /><br />Anyway. I'M SORRY. I know lots of people love this book and I'm not sitting here comparing it to John's books because they're so so different. <b>I just didn't mesh with this at all lmao what was this.</b> </span>

<div id="review_2553942510" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2553942510" />
    <a title="Sol ~ TheBookishKing" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/69919640-sol-thebookishking"><img alt="Sol ~ TheBookishKing" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1596427937p2/69919640.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2553942510?book_show_action=true">Oct 06, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sol ~ TheBookishKing" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sol ~ TheBookishKing" href="/user/show/69919640-sol-thebookishking">Sol ~ TheBookishKing</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2553942510" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17377210496107811529">Buddy Read with <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2551358279?book_show_action=false' rel='nofollow noopener'>Fellow Cardan the Furry Enthusiast</a><br/><br/><i>
  <b>"Carl is not a possible thing, and yet there he is, guarding the Chipotle, leading people to conclude that he was not created by humans."</b>
</i><br/><br/>So ... just like I thought ... Trash.<br/><br/>I went in to this with semi high hopes as everyone says that Hank Green is the Better Brother due to the fact that John just keeps putting out Not So Great Books. There was tons of hype around this book that I was gladly not involved in but I read this in favor of a frien</span>
  <span id="freeText17377210496107811529" style="display:none">Buddy Read with <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2551358279?book_show_action=false" rel="nofollow noopener">Fellow Cardan the Furry Enthusiast</a><br /><br /><i>
  <b>"Carl is not a possible thing, and yet there he is, guarding the Chipotle, leading people to conclude that he was not created by humans."</b>
</i><br /><br />So ... just like I thought ... Trash.<br /><br />I went in to this with semi high hopes as everyone says that Hank Green is the Better Brother due to the fact that John just keeps putting out Not So Great Books. There was tons of hype around this book that I was gladly not involved in but I read this in favor of a friend who adores Hank but wanted thoughts on the book before she bought it. So here I've been for the past two days being extremely conflicted.<br /><br />The beginning to this book was rough, I didn't care for the characters but the story line was kind of interesting and kept me going. Then I started to love the middle a lot and was incredibly interested in everything that was happening! It was crazy hard to put down and I was devouring it ............ then came Chapter 13 - Chapter 25. They completely ruined this amazing idea and I am still a tad angry.<br /><br />An Absolutely Remarkable Thing is about April, a normal girl who lives in New York, working an insanely annoying App coding job. It is her life, despite completely despising it. One night on her way home, she passes the local Chipotle and a large Transformer Robot Like structure is just standing there. Just thinking it's a piece of art, she calls a friend and together they film a video about this amazing piece of art that may not be exactly a piece of art. Together they name him Carl. <br /><br />When April awakes the next morning, she is insanely famous for her discovery of the Carls. But New York Carl isn't the only one like his kind, all over the world Carls have appeared. The world is now changed forever by the appearance of these robots, and Aprils life is changed forever as she takes in being a famous discoverer. <br /><br />This book explores the topics of social structures bought upon becoming internet famous, brings about the struggles of the media industry and really the falsities that we are taught about being famous. Which I thought was really cool because Hank was here to EXPOSE the world and I was living, especially since he knows pretty well what it's like to be Internet Famous, practically everyone knows the Greens. So not only was this exploring social media life and internet fame, we also got to see this science robot - potential alien side. All of this mixed together is actually really really good and I loved this part so much.<br /><br />But here's where I started to see more John Green and Less of Hank. Okay also don't get me wrong, I went into this book looking for Hank Green as an author. I didn't want to compare him to his brother at all BUT it just happened because I believe their writing styles are actually very very similar. <br /><br />We have here another boring white girl who believes herself to be so different because she doesn't like Twitter and visits Art Museums instead. But whereas John Green features hopeless straight whites needing to find their tragic lover, Hank features a Bisexual Main Character and a Sapphic Relationship. <br /><br />Hank differs from his brother I believe in, wanting to explore and break normal social gender roles. Which is great of course and I loved but he killed it for me with April by making the only thing that didn't make her your normal Green hopeless romantic white girl, is that she's bisexual. She literally says she isn't like other girls because she doesn't like twitter and goes to Art Museums. Like wow I'm so dang impressed April you're SO QUIRKY I'm so PROUD OF YOU. And it bothered me a lot.<br /><br />What really killed this book though is the Pretentious Ending that made me want to pull my hair out and scream for years. As I said, I didn't want to compare the brothers against each other because I just think it's wrong and I wanted to give him a chance ... but okay were him and John both taught that Pretentious Endings are the Key to a Good Book? The ending tries to explain that we as Humans are more important than we think possible. And OKay sure I'm here for a good "We all need to be here for each other as humans and bring each other up," story line but not when it is randomly thrown in and ruins a GOOOD story line.<br /><br />I cannot STAND this whole we as humans are so much smarter than we believe and we need to believe in our uniqueness because YES we're amazing. Sure we are, but DO NOT build up this book about science and potential aliens and robots and fame and hollywood ONLY to end it on Garbage. It's like the whole middle of this book was thrown out so our Pretentious Overlord could deal this crap onto us.<br /><br />N O W, I do think a lot of people will absolutely love this. But as someone who was read John Greens books for over 5+ years and having to deal with ridiculous characters who think they're so quirky because they don't do normal things, I'm just tired. I'm real tired of this stuff y'all. <br /><br /><b>THE ENDING THAT MADE ME ANGRY. IT'S SPOILERY SO BEWARE!</b><br />So of course these large robots are aliens. Sentient Beings from across the Universe. And the Carls are sending infectious Dreams through humans minds, making us solve a puzzle. But April is special, she is the chosen ones of the Carls. SO at the end right, she ends up dying because QUIRKY April must have a tragic death. And when she dies she goes into this dream state where she sees Carl and he explains to her that THE CARLS CAME DOWN to unite humans together in being good humans. And to remind US that we are important and unique and beautiful. So you want me to believe this whole time that you built up a story revolving around science, social constructs, fame, and aliens to end it like that?! These huge robot SENTIENT beings from OUTER SPACE came to remind us Humans that are we important? Exit the building please because I am not here for this garbage. It didn't even go well with the story line and here I am BEING angry because it just is pretentious as heck and .. why did I even waste my time.<br /><br />SO YEAH THERES THAT REVIEW. It's a mess but I have been beyond ignored all day and angry so I had to vent out this review.<br /><br />Thank you for coming to my TED Talk.</span>
  <a data-text-id="17377210496107811529" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2274201861?book_show_action=true">Nov 06, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Annemarie" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Annemarie" href="/user/show/27544490-annemarie">Annemarie</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/27544490-annemarie?shelf=read-in-english">read-in-english</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2274201861" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3482224059762501804"><b>Actual rating: 3.5 🌟's</b><br/><br/>I initially gave this book 5 stars, because I had such an amazing time reading it and was so incredibly surprised by Hank Green's writing talent. It's hard to believe that this is his debut novel! The writing style was super easy to read and the teenagers actually talked like teenagers. It's nice to know that there are authors out there, who don't insist on constantly using stupid slang to get someones youth across. <br/><br/>I found the characters to be lovable and realistic, but wa</span>
  <span id="freeText3482224059762501804" style="display:none"><b>Actual rating: 3.5 🌟's</b><br /><br />I initially gave this book 5 stars, because I had such an amazing time reading it and was so incredibly surprised by Hank Green's writing talent. It's hard to believe that this is his debut novel! The writing style was super easy to read and the teenagers actually talked like teenagers. It's nice to know that there are authors out there, who don't insist on constantly using stupid slang to get someones youth across. <br /><br />I found the characters to be lovable and realistic, but was left waiting for more background information on them. I think that's the reason why I forgot a lot of the story quickly after I finished reading. I lowered my rating because of this, because in the end, nothing was truly <i>remarkable</i>. <br /><br /><b>However</b>, the plot is still something really unique and unlike anything I've read before. It certainly was a very creative story that was well executed overall and also included some <u>wonderful</u> quotes. It was a super fun and fast read, and its one of those books I definitely see myself rereading at some point (I guess it actually will be a good thing I forgot a lot already - I will be surprised at the twists and turns again!) I'm excited for the sequel!</span>
  <a data-text-id="3482224059762501804" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2128848845" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2128848845" />
    <a title="Kaylin (The Re-Read Queen)" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/47627265-kaylin-the-re-read-queen"><img alt="Kaylin (The Re-Read Queen)" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1560356189p2/47627265.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2128848845?book_show_action=true">Sep 19, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kaylin (The Re-Read Queen)" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kaylin (The Re-Read Queen)" href="/user/show/47627265-kaylin-the-re-read-queen">Kaylin (The Re-Read Queen)</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/47627265-kaylin-the-re-read-queen?shelf=speculative-lit-not-quite-sci-fi">speculative-lit-not-quite-sci-fi</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/47627265-kaylin-the-re-read-queen?shelf=lgbt">lgbt</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/47627265-kaylin-the-re-read-queen?shelf=didn-t-finish">didn-t-finish</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2128848845" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4590128821745019891"><b>DNF @ 75% </b><br/><br/><i>
  <blockquote> “Behold the field in which I grow my fucks. Lay thine eyes upon it and see that it is barren.” </blockquote>
</i><br/><br/>Me: *eagerly pre-ordered this book over a year ago*<br/>Me: *didn’t read it until my book-club picked it as BOM*<br/>Me: *Didn’t finish said book*<br/>Me: *Left an unfinished “It’s-not-you-it’s-me” review for over 9 months*<br/>Me: <br/><br/><span>[image error]</span> <br/><br/>Honestly, this is not a <i>bad</i> book. It talks about the internet in a non-demonizing way. There’s discussion of fame, power dynamics, ethics vs celebrity and all this </span>
  <span id="freeText4590128821745019891" style="display:none"><b>DNF @ 75% </b><br><br><i>
  <blockquote> “Behold the field in which I grow my fucks. Lay thine eyes upon it and see that it is barren.” </blockquote>
</i><br><br>Me: *eagerly pre-ordered this book over a year ago*<br>Me: *didn’t read it until my book-club picked it as BOM*<br>Me: *Didn’t finish said book*<br>Me: *Left an unfinished “It’s-not-you-it’s-me” review for over 9 months*<br>Me: <br><br><span>[image error]</span> <br><br>Honestly, this is not a <i>bad</i> book. It talks about the internet in a non-demonizing way. There’s discussion of fame, power dynamics, ethics vs celebrity and all this really interesting really <i>relevant stuff.</i><br><br><b>And the author is in a unique position to talk about this stuff?</b> As someone who clambered to internet fame before we really realized that was a <i>thing</i> Hank Green (and his brother John) have done amazing things with their evolving platform-- CrashCourse for dozens of school subjects, Sci-Show for my science geeks, hilarious vlogbrother videos and those that TLDR: current events. I still regularly listen to two of Hank Green’s podcasts (Delete This and Dear Hank and John, which if you liked this I <i>highly</i> recommend). Hank Green literally has a whole podcast where he and his wife go through his twitter each week and discuss how this form of communication hinders and helps in politics, environmentalism and basic empathy-- a theme in this book. Aka <b> this book covers stuff the author really, really cares about, and it shows.</b><br><br>But maybe that’s part of why it didn’t work for me. This narrative voice didn’t feel like a new character to me. It sounded just like Hank Green. <b>This really may be just a case of me having already read/seen/listened to too much of the author himself. </b>But it made the book feel lackluster to me. <br><br><b>In Conclusion:</b><br><br>In all actuality a case of “it’s-not-you-it’s-me” but if I wanted to hear a narrative voice that sounded just like Hank Green talk about his thoughts on the internet and fame…. I’d just listen to one of his podcasts.<br><br><br>——————<br><br>*puts on spectacles*<br><br>TIME TO IMAGINE A ROBOT COMPLEXLY 🤖 <br><br><br><br>.<br>.<br>.<br><br><br>I knew it! <br><br>I still listen to Hank and John Green's podcast, and a few episodes ago Hank teased an announcement. Totally nailed it with my guess it was going to be a book! <br><br>Love both the brothers and the impact they've had on Internet culture. Hank is especially well-spoken and enthusiastic about so many important things, can't wait to see how this translates into a book!</span>
